==PAGE:5==
 
 
Abstract 
 
The present study had the objective of implementing a model of the behavior of plastic additives 
in the European value chain. The system set up for the year 2014 and is composed of manufacturing 
stages, use phase of several product categories and waste management processes. The model applies the 
methodology of Material Flow Analysis to calculate flows using mass balance within the system and 
migration modelling to calculate the release of additive from plastic products. The resulting system 
contains a layer of polymer and multiple substance layers. The resulting flows are detailed in the case 
of cadmium embedded in polypropylene. The results show high release levels for the use phase of 
several products as well as for landfilled plastic waste and highlight the necessity of strengthening 
regulations of potentially hazardous chemicals to lower public exposure. The model’s strengths and 
weaknesses are reviewed to allow transparency in the use of the program and the interpretation of the 
results.

1. Introduction 
 
Polymerized materials, commonly known as plastics, have become widely produced and used 
since their invention 70 years ago. In Europe, plastics is a great part of the economy, responsible for 1.5 
million jobs and an annual turnover of 350 billion euros in 2017 (PlasticsEurope, 2018). Plastics are 
present in a wide variety of designs and shapes in almost all sectors of the economy. In 2017, 64.4 
million tonnes of plastics were produced in Europe, representing a 7.3% increase from 2016 
(PlasticsEurope, 2018). 
The polymer industry is meeting a high demand in many different areas of the economy. A large 
amount of chemicals can be mixed with polymer materials to alter their properties. These additives are 
widely used by the industry to produce plastic materials with properties specific to their use. They can 
give functional improvement (e.g. flame retardants, stabilizers), reduce production costs (e.g. fillers) or 
make a product more engaging (e.g. colorants).The high demand on various products and functionalities 
combined with the versatility and ease of polymer materials to be mixed with different components is 
responsible for a vast spectrum of different materials. 
However, the production of plastic impacts the environment and human health. From an 
environmental point of view, plastic materials are persistent  and accumulate in several ecosystems. This 
is particularly notorious in marine ecosystems where plastic litter brought in high quantity from the coast 
and rivers is estimated to 80-85% of the total number of marine litter items. In the ocean, plastics are to 
a large extent degraded by solar radiation and mechanical forces, progressively forming microplastics, 
but can also sink to depths  where no such conditions occur and thus increase their persistency (Jamieson 
et al., 2019). In terrestrial ecosystems, the effects are not as mediatized as in the marine and freshwater 
ecosystems but are still present (de Souza Machado et al., 2018). The same mechanisms of degradation 
occur, and microplastics travelling with weather conditions are widely scattered and possibly ingested 
by the fauna (Chae and An, 2018). These mechanisms can alter the chemical property of the soil and 
waters and most importantly alter human activities and health. A few examples are degraded landscapes, 
harm of tourism via visual pollution and extra costs from clean-up of beaches and other terrains and 
health issues caused by pollution of drinking water and agricultural and fishing activities.  
One of the main issues regarding the complexity of plastic products and the wastes generated is 
the difficulty in recycling the plastic as it hampers the industrial recovery of polymer from plastic waste. 
Another problematic is the fact that recovered polymers can contain unknown amounts of chemicals, 
thus complicating the sorting and recovery of plastics. Furthermore, an important aspect of the chemical 
pollution caused by plastic materials is the presence of additives in polymers that can be released in the 
environment during the use-phase of products or when littered (Lithner, 2011; Hansen et al., 2013).  
==PAGE:13==
 
 
14
 
The above mentioned problems have led to EU-wide legislation to limit plastic pollution, among 
which market restrictions on single use plastics, the use of chemicals contained in plastics, as well as 
the promotion of recycling (European Commission, 2018). Concerning the chemical composition, the 
European parliament has regulated the use of additives in the production of plastic commodities 
(REACH. Registration Evaluation Authorisation and Restriction of Chemicals., 2006). These 
regulations, said to be applied in accordance with the Precautionary Principle (Council of the European 
Union, 2006; Hansen, Carlsen and Tickner, 2007), aim at limiting the legal quantities to protect 
consumer health and the environment (Turner, 2019). 
A study of the migration of these chemicals is all the more important as their behaviour is not 
systematically known. They can potentially leak into the environment, causing harm to natural 
ecosystem, pollute water sources or cause health concerns when ingested or when in contact with skin 
tissues. It is also possible that the chemicals, instead of escaping from plastic objects, accumulate during 
successive recycling processes in the lifetime of a plastic product, causing the concentration of additive 
in plastic products to rise in time. This increase in concentration could increase the public’s exposure to 
potentially hazardous substances. 
The matter of migration of chemical from plastics has been studied in several articles. A 
thorough review of polymers and additives regarding their migration has been conducted by COWI in 
2013 (Hansen et al., 2013) and Hahladakis et al (2018). However, the matter is almost exclusively 
regarded concerning food and skin contact. This is the case for experimental measurements (Hosogai et 
al., 1992; Richardson, 2004; Cai, Nthoiwa and Ge, 2014; Gontard, 2014) and for models (Begley et al., 
2005; Beldì et al., 2012a; Kiyataka, Dantas and Pallone, 2015). Other studies have focused on different 
parts of the products life cycles and on different materials. For instance, Pivnenko, Eriksen, et al., (2016) 
focused on the fate of selected chemicals during the waste management processes of paper products. 
The study of a full life cycle is yet to be done. 
The objective of this project is to analyse the behaviour and fate of chemical additives 
throughout the life cycle of plastic products in Europe. This is done by : (i) reviewing literature, selecting 
relevant chemicals ; (ii) building a model assessing diffusion in different settings  ; (iii) conducting a 
material flow analysis (MFA)  ; (iv) assessing the uncertainties of the MFA results using a Monte Carlo 
analysis  
 
 
==PAGE:14==
 
 
15
More specifically, this project aims at building a model based on a multi-layer Material Flow 
Analysis approach combining the flows of plastic commodities and embedded chemicals additives. The 
model is a process by process calculation of the additive’s migration based on two diffusion models. It 
is based on the diffusion of chemicals through the polymer matrix and its progressive escape during the 
plastic lifetime. The system parameters correspond to Europe for the year 2014. The processes 
considered include the production phase, the use phase and the end-of-life phase of several product 
categories. 
Emphasis is put on the transparency of the model, the goal being to allow users to apply it to 
different polymers and chemical additives, as well as to different geographical and temporal settings. 
All the parameters are stored in accessible databases, allowing the user to verify and change them for 
future projects. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:15==
 
 
16
2. Background 
2.1. 
Plastics 
Plastics are defined as a synthetic material produced from a mix of organic polymers and 
additives that can be moulded and shaped into different products (Oxford University Dictionary, 2018). 
Plastics are known to have been used throughout history, for instance by Egyptians as glue. In its modern 
form, artificial synthetic plastic were first developed by the Hyatt brothers with the invention of celluloid 
in 1870 (Curley, Das and Young, 2018). From its access to the market in 1910 (Waring, 2017), the 
consumption of plastics has grown exponentially and was estimated to 348 million tonnes globally in 
2017 (Plastics Europe, 2018). Its use has grown to include a large number of common items as well as 
industrial commodities, as seen in Figure 1. 
 
Figure 1. Total plastics demand by market sector in 2017 (Plastics Europe, 2018) 
Plastics play an important role in the increase of life standards globally, allowing access to cheap 
products being able to fulfil many purposes, from chirurgical tools to sewer pipes. Its economic impact 
is also major. The decrease in costs of most of the facilities and commodity products used by household, 
industries and public entities have boosted the economy.

2.1.1. 
Plastics and polymers 
Plastic material is the result of mixing a polymer with certain additives. Polymers are formed 
by bounding together monomeric chains during the so-called polymerization process to form long 
polymeric chains. Monomers, molecules linked together into large chains, are the basic units of the 
polymer and for the most part obtained from crude oil distillation. The polymerization process is based 
on the atoms' ability to bound together to form larger chains. The most common structure includes a 
backbone chain of carbon atoms on which other radicals are attached to give different types of polymers. 
This process, which involves a chemical reaction between the monomers with high temperature, 
pressure and catalysts (Shrivastava, 2018), can vary from one industrial company to another. 
Polymers can take multiple forms based on four main formations (Riley, 2012) :  
• 
Linear polymer: Long, straight chain of monomers 
• 
Branched polymer: Division of a linear chain into a ramified chain. Linear polymer chains can 
contain short branches. 
• 
Cross-linked polymer: Some monomers have more than two reactive sites and can form cross-
links when binding together, forming a network. They form strong structures that resist 
temperature and chemical stress. 
• 
Co-polymer: A polymer made if more than one type of monomer. It allows controlling the 
polymer propriety by adjustment of the ratio of the monomer types. 
Thermosetting and thermoplastic polymers are the two main groups of polymers. 
Thermosetting polymers can be formed of branched or linear polymer chains and with no direct 
connection between polymer chains. With high temperature, the weak bonds between the chains loosen 
and as chains become independent, the polymer can be reshaped. When the temperature is lowered, the 
weak bonds reform and the new shape is maintained. This allows thermosetting polymers to be reused 
by mechanical recycling. 
Thermoplastic polymers are formed by cross-link connections which makes melting and thus 
mechanical recycling impossible. Thermosetting polymers acquire their final shape in the production 
phase and cannot be reshaped. It can be recycled with the so-called feedstock recycling process which 
consists of a chemical depolymerisation of the plastics material. However, they have different 
proprieties that justify their use, although produced in smaller quantities. Both types of polymers are 
shown in  
Table 1.

2.1.2. 
Plastic manufacturing 
Plastics are manufactured from a polymer and a mix of additives. The polymer gives the 
structure and the main characteristics of the plastics and is chosen depending on the application of the 
plastics, while the additives give a finishing touch of practical and aesthetic attributes. Overall, plastic 
manufacturing is divided into five major steps, each including several possible processes depending on 
the type of polymer, additives, facilities, and available technology or on the desired characteristics of 
the plastics. The following description will be concise as the aim is to give a clear idea of the plastic 
manufacturing process rather than losing the reader in detail that won’t be used in the main report. 
The first step of plastics manufacturing is the reception and handling of the materials. Raw 
polymer molecules in dry, powder or liquid form as well as additives are transported and received at the 
manufacturing plant. 
 
==PAGE:18==
 
 
19
The second step is the polymerization. As described in the section above, the polymer 
molecules, or monomers, are connected into long chains during a chemical process. In this step, the 
process type, the temperature and the pressure applied to the monomers influence the chemical reaction 
and are used to control the proprieties of the polymer. 
The third step is compounding. In this step, the additives are mixed with the polymer. It is 
usually carried out in an independent process after polymerisation but can also be performed 
simultaneously.  
The fourth step is Conversion. It aims at giving the plastics material its final shape. Various 
processes can be used to obtain the desired shape and they depend on the nature of the polymer as 
thermoplastics and thermosets use different processes. Thermoplastics processes involve melting the 
plastics material, shaping it and then cooling it to keep its form. Various processes can be used of which 
Injection moulding and film extrusion are dominating as shown in Table 2 
For thermosetting, less processes are available. The most used process is a compression 
moulding process. (Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development, 2014)

2.2. 
Additives in plastics materials 
The chemical additives used in plastics products can be divided into four categories: functional 
additives, colorants, fillers and reinforcements (Hansen, Nilsson and Ravnholt Vium, 2014)  
2.2.1. 
Categories of additives  
Functional additives are used to give practical characteristics to the polymer in order to increase 
their lifetime (e.g. stabiliser, antistatic agents), give them specific characteristic to fulfil their purpose 
(e.g. plasticsizers, lubricants, slip agents, curing agents, foaming agents) and to lower health risks (e.g. 
flame retardants, biocides). 
Colorants for plastic products include among others organic and inorganic pigments and dyes. 
Fillers are added to increase the stiffness to the product or to reduce the costs of production as 
they are cheaper than polymer materials in average (Ceresana, 2019). They can include mica, talc, 
kaolin, clay, calcium carbonate and barium sulphate. 
Reinforcement affects the mechanical properties of the polymer by affecting the resistance to 
tensile strength and shrinkage among others (Santa Clara University and Engineering Design Center, 
2006). They include for instance glass and carbon fibres. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:20==
 
 
21
2.2.2. 
Migration 
The components used as additives in plastics materials can potentially have negative effects 
when in contact with the human skin, food or beverages or when escaping into the environment. These 
additives have the ability to migrate from the polymer matrix to a solvent present in food, beverage, skin 
or air in contact with the plastics. The phenomenon of migration is defined by the diffusion of a chemical 
compound from a higher concentration area to a lower concentration area, producing a flux of chemicals 
from the polymer matrix to the solvent.  
While migration is inevitable, the rate at which a chemical compound can reach a solvent can 
vary greatly (Bhunia et al., 2013). The relative amount of the additive in regard to the polymer, the 
chemical bound of the additive with the polymer or the temperature are parameters that influence that 
rate. 
Migration can be divided into four major steps. 
1. The diffusion of the compound through the polymer matrix following a concentration gradient 
2. The desorption, or release of the molecules from the surface of the polymer.  
3. The sorption of the molecules at the interface of the solvent 
4. The absorption of the molecules by the solvent 
A fifth step, which does not concern the migration per se, would be the diffusion of the 
compound throughout the solvent. 
The migration potential of an additive from a specific polymer to a specific solvent in a specific 
situation is usually determined by laboratory experiments. The aim is to determine in controlled 
experiments how additives migrate in the use phase when in contact with the human skin, sensitive 
zones such as the lips or infants’ skin or with food or beverages that could be ingested. A considerable 
literature review for migration experiments is presented in (Hahladakis et al., 2018), concluding that the 
specific conditions of the experiments (“the nature of food, the food-additive interactions and time–
temperature-storage conditions”) significantly affect the migration mechanisms. 
An alternative approach based on modelling is also possible. Migration is essentially governed 
by Fick’s laws (Crank, 1975) stating that: 
𝐽= −𝐷𝑃
𝜕𝐶𝑃
𝜕𝑥

and

These equations have been used by numerous authors to describe the behaviour of additives in 
polymers (Reynier, Dole and Feigenbaum, 2002; Begley et al., 2005; Simoneau, 2010; Bhunia et al., 
2013) and will be described in detail in the following chapters. 
2.3. 
Plastic waste.  
.The large production of plastics globally has created an important pollution issue over the years 
and the understanding of its magnitude is constantly growing, driven by the increasing costs of 
landfilling and the emerging environmental issues. Polymers are hardly biodegradable and are therefore 
persistent in nature. Their properties and in particular their solidity and light weight make them difficult 
to store without escaping and allows them to travel long distances with wind and water streams. 
Furthermore, the large number of polymer types makes them difficult to sort and to include in the overall 
waste management. It is known to cause an array of issues. Visual pollution is caused by littering in 
natural or inhabited places or by plastics traveling with the wind or currents. Plastics can impact animal 
health, and therefore biodiversity, by ingestion or by causing physical handicap. The mechanical 
degradation of plastics into microplastics invisible to the naked eye and spreading in virtually all habitats 
and accumulating in the food chain, potentially reaching human ingestion. Finally, the leak of chemical 
additives is potentially responsible for health and environmental issues when in contact with humans 
and the environment. 
To face these concerns and mitigate them, an efficient waste management is crucial. The 
European Union has recently developed a scheme aiming at reducing plastics in landfills, encouraging 
recycling and act at the consumer level by prohibiting certain single-use commodities and plastic bags 
as well as educating consumers (European Commission, 2018). 
==PAGE:22==
 
 
23
2.3.1. 
Waste management 
Today’s plastic waste management system consists of sorting plastics materials and is divided 
into material and energy recovery. The fraction that is not sorted away from other waste materials or 
that is not recovered for economical or practical reasons is traditionally landfilled or incinerated along 
with other waste types. 
After being collected, plastic wastes are separated by polymer type by manual or automated 
sorting. This process is crucial to keep a close to optimal recovery quality. It consists in separating the 
polymer types using the identification system showed in Table 3. Furthermore, non-plastics elements 
such as paper labels or sorting errors are removed as far as possible. The recovery methods employed 
depend on the political and economic context and thus depend on the geography. A few examples on 
how are European countries manage their plastic waste is presented in Table 4. 
Table 3. Five first plastic resin identification codes defined by the European Commission along with

2.3.2. 
Plastics recycling 
Landfilling plastic waste is the traditional way of dealing with plastic materials and is still 
common in Europe (Hopewell, Dvorak and Kosior, 2009; PlasticsEurope, 2018). This is due to the low 
price of plastics and their inherent complexity, making them difficult to reprocess while easy to 
manufacture. As shown in Table 4, in Europe, the post treatment of plastic is not equal for all countries. 
However, various elements now contribute to a decreased use of landfilling, with a 40% decrease from 
==PAGE:23==
 
 
24
2006 to 2016 (PlasticsEurope, 2018). Among them, the financial reasons coming from the price increase 
of petrol and energy, the cost decrease of sorting and recycling, the lack of space for landfilling and the 
environmental awareness of the public putting pressure on governments and companies producing or 
selling plastics products (Hahladakis et al., 2018; PlasticsEurope, 2018). However, the overall trend  
Table 4. Post-consumer plastics waste treatment in 2016 (PlasticsEurope, 2018)

44.1% 
 
Plastics recovery can be divided into primary, secondary, tertiary and quaternary recycling 
(Ignatyev et al., 2014). Recycling plastics necessitates important steps. The first one is the sorting 
process, in which the waste in separated depending on the polymer type. The second one is the cleaning 
process, in which the impurities are removed. These two steps have the common aim of making the 
recyclate as pure as possible and thus keeping a maximum of its original properties. 
2.3.2.1. Primary recycling 
Primary recycling involves the use of clean plastics to be transformed into a new product without 
any loss in properties. As such, it mainly affects the pre-consumer plastics in closed-loop recycling 
within the plastics manufacturing and before the product’s access to the market. However, post-
consumer plastics, which involve the materials after being use by e.g. households, can be included in 
primary recycling following an expensive sorting and cleaning process (Christensen, 2011). Closed-
loop recycling allows close to no cleaning and sorting process as the material has not been in contact to 
other products and the use of the recycled polymer almost as virgin polymer. As this step is performed 
through mechanical recycling, it can only concern thermoplastics (Christensen, 2011; Ignatyev et al., 
2014). 
 
 
==PAGE:24==
 
 
25
2.3.2.2. Secondary recycling 
Secondary recycling involves the plastics discarded after the use phase. It consists in mechanical 
recycling. The sorting and cleaning processes are essential in secondary recycling as the quality and the 
mechanical properties of the resulting plastic materials depend on the quality of the processed waste. 
This drop in quality is referred to as downcycling. It is caused by contamination of the main polymer by 
other polymers, impurities and water (Ignatyev et al., 2014; Paine, Rae and Blanksby, 2014; Ragaert, 
Delva and Van Geem, 2017).  
2.3.2.3. Tertiary recycling 
In tertiary recycling, also referred as feedstock recycling, the polymers are broken down into 
smaller molecules. The polymer can either be broken down into monomers through depolymerisation 
or into different products, usually liquids and gases, that can be used to produce fuels, new polymers or 
other chemicals. However, this process is not financially optimal as it is costly compared to the raw 
materials it aims at substituting (Ignatyev et al., 2014). 
2.3.2.4. Quaternary recycling 
Quaternary recycling refers to incineration. This method allows to recover energy from the 
plastic waste. It is an efficient way to dispose of contaminated waste or waste for which no other 
recycling method is practically or financially possible. This method can be used in industrial processes 
such as cement furnaces or metal melting ovens but also to generate electricity and heat for residential 
areas (Al-Salem, Lettieri and Baeyens, 2010). While incineration of plastics emits greenhouse gases 
from the combustion of polymers, the energy recovered is still considered to have negative emission due 
to the avoided fuel that would otherwise be necessary to produce the same amount of energy (Eriksson 
and Finnveden, 2009). 
In the case of biodegradable polymers, which are not covered in this thesis, the biological 
degradation can be considered as a recycling process. 
When it comes to the optimal recycling solution, many factors must be taken into consideration. 
The fact that plastics are an essential part of the society and living standards as well as the fact that 
sorting and recycling processes have limitations are a few of them. A balance between a moderated and 
smarter consumption and a thorough waste management can be considered a legitimate solution 
(Ignatyev et al., 2014). 
 
 
==PAGE:25==
 
 
26
3. Methodology 
3.1. 
Overall methodology 
This project investigates the additive content in polymer products throughout their lifetime and 
the losses and accumulations that can be observed. The flow of additives in the value chain is determined 
based on various parameters that interact with each other following specific equations. The study follows 
the principles and methodologies of Material Flow Analysis. 
The MFA methodology is based on the principle of mass conservation and the determination of 
flows within defined system boundaries. The MFA system is divided in processes linked together by 
flows representing the transport of matter, goods or substances from one process to another. By assessing 
and comparing all known inputs, stocks and outputs of all processes, this modelling method allows to 
reconcile and quantify the whole system using mass balance and making visible the accumulation or 
depletion of material. The principle of mass conservation states that for a specific parameter, there 
cannot be more matter going out than going in, i.e. the total stock cannot be negative. However, for a 
given period in time, the stocks in a process can decrease for outflows larger than the inflows (∆𝑆𝑡𝑜𝑐𝑘 
negative) and increase in the opposite situation (∆𝑆𝑡𝑜𝑐𝑘 positive), This principle of mass balance is 
represented by the following equation: 
∑𝐼𝑛𝑓𝑙𝑜𝑤𝑠= ∑𝑂𝑢𝑡𝑓𝑙𝑜𝑤𝑠+ ∆𝑆𝑡𝑜𝑐𝑘
(𝐸𝑞 3) 
The MFA methodology is applied in strict boundaries of space and time. For the same system, 
different layers of materials can be considered. Each layer describes a particular good or substance 
traveling through the same flows and processes but in potentially different amounts. In this report, a 
layer of polymer and a layer of chemical additive is calculated. The first layer describes the overall life 
cycle of a polymer type inside a time and geographical frame and the second describes the behaviour of 
the chemical additive embedded in the polymer.  
The processes that make up the system are adapted from the study of Kawecki, Scheeder and 
Nowack (2018) which assesses the flows of seven different polymers following a static probabilistic 
MFA (PMFA). The PMFA study shown in Figure 2 is set for the European Union and Switzerland 
separately and for the year 2014. It includes the production of plastic from polymer, the use phase for 
different product sectors, waste collection systems and treatments, including recycling. However, it does 
not consider the share of recycled and reusable material going back into the system but rather the total 
amount recovered going out of the system and the total amount of recovered material going into the 
system, regardless of the share coming from the recycling processes within the system and out of the 
==PAGE:26==
 
 
27
system. Moreover, this model does not account for stocks. This limitation will be discussed further in 
the discussion section in part 5.2.3 
 
Figure 2. General structure of the Material Flow model as shown in Kawecki, Scheeder and Nowack (2018). 
The present report aims at assessing the flows of chemical additives present in plastic products 
from various polymer types. The calculation is based on the MFA methodology and thus respects the 
principle of mass balance. For each process, the outflows are calculated from the known inflows. To 
calculate the migration taking place for each process, specific mathematical functions based on Fick’s 
laws of diffusion are used (Fick, 1855).  
To calculate these flows, a model combining the mass balance approach and the migration 
modelling is developed. The algorithm operating these calculations is coded on the MATLAB 
programming language and is made of a Main script calling several other scripts to perform specific 
tasks. For each flow of polymer from a process to another, the mass of additive embedded in the polymer 
is calculated as well as the mass of additive released from the polymer due to the conditions in the 
processes.  
 
==PAGE:27==
 
 
28
The next section goes over the two models used to calculate the release of additive from plastic 
products. Part 3.3 gives an overview of what the algorithm developed in this project require as inputs 
and what it delivers as output is given. Part 3.4  provides a detailed description of how the algorithm is 
working is made. Finally, Part 3.5 and Part 3.6 provide an overview of how the data was collected and 
how the uncertainty analysis was performed are given. 
3.2. 
Migration functions 
 
As described in the Background part, a plastic material is manufactured from a polymer and a 
mix of additives and these additives will have a tendency to migrate throughout the lifecycle of the 
plastic material. To account for that migration, each process in the system is a part of the polymer’s 
lifecycle and the amount of additive that migrates out of the plastic material is calculated for each 
process. While various models can be used to calculate the share of additive leaving the plastic material, 
they are all resulting from Fick’s laws (Crank, 1975; Piringer and Baner, 2000; Simoneau, 2010; OECD, 
2014). In this project, two diffusion models are used, both derived from Crank's (1975) research on 
Fick’s laws solutions. The first one is given by Simoneau (2010) and is valid for food contact. The 
second one is given by the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development (2014) and is 
valid for emissions to air. Both models will be explained in detail in the following sections. 
The main components of the algorithm developed in this project are the migration functions. 
They calculate the amount of chemical that is released from a plastic material based on several 
parameters.  
Currently existing migration estimation models are based on Fick’s laws of diffusion. The key 
parameter is the coefficient of diffusion of the migrating material in the containing material giving the 
magnitude of a substance’s motion through a material. The existing migration models are used as worst-
case migration predictions.  
To calculate the migration in a specific process, two migration functions can be called: 
Simoneau.m for food contact situations and OECD.m for air contact situations. For both functions an 
important assumption is the simplicity of the objects’ geometry. All are represented as cuboids of 
volume V=Area*depth. In the food contact situation, the area of contact is considered as one of the faces 
of the cuboid whereas in the air contact situation, two faces are considered. 
 
 
==PAGE:28==
 
 
29

Simoneau (2010), using the analytical solution from Crank (1975) for monolayer homogeneous 
materials. It can be expressed by the following equation elaborated by Simoneau (2010):

3.3. 
Parameters 
3.3.1. 
Inputs 
The algorithm developed in this project to calculate the substance flows requires several inputs. It is 
built so that all necessary parameters need to be given and the opposite would result in an error. 
• 
The first elements to input are linked to the type of polymer and chemical additive chosen. These 
parameters describe the physicochemical composition of the materials the model will be 
working with. These include the density of the polymer and the additive, the molar mass of the 
additive and polymer specific diffusion parameters. They are not given with an uncertainty as 
the literature gives very little details on their uncertainty but are usually given with high 
resolution (i.e. more than three significant digits). However, they may vary with specific 
conditions not considered in this project such as pressure. 
• 
The mass flows of plastic products are used as the first layer of the MFA and allow to derive 
the mass of chemicals from concentrations. The flows are taken from Kawecki, Scheeder and 
Nowack (2018) and are displayed in the form of a table. To be used by the algorithm, the flows 
need to be displayed in a matrix form. This transformation is detailed in part 3.4.1. 
• 
A description of the physicochemical conditions for each process. These parameters include the 
temperature and the average time the product will stay in the process as well as the average 
dimensions of the products in terms of thickness and area of contact. These parameters are 
averages of a high amount of different possible situations and thus have a high dispersion. The 
values are typically found in specialized literature or datasets as well as industrial data as 
detailed in part 3.5. 
• 
The initial concentration of the additive contained in each product is necessary to get the initial 
mass of the additive in each particular commodity. This parameter is difficult to obtain with 
high accuracy. The data on the manufacturers’ and the literature’s sides are very scarce and the 
values when found might not be representative:  
==PAGE:32==
 
 
33
- 
Values regarding specific commodities and thus not representative of a category of 
commodities (i.e. a specific piece of furniture not being representative of the whole 
furniture product) 
- 
Not representative in terms of time (ex: legal threshold got drastically lowered in a 
short period of time in the EU and thus data from 2010 might not be representative of 
today’s situation) 
- 
Not representative in terms of geography (ex: Data found for products that may not 
enter the European market for safety or economic reasons) 
To get around these limitations, the values chosen as initial concentration were set in the 
following way. 
- 
The initial concentration is the same for manufacturing processes (i.e. all products on 
the market will have the same concentration of the studied chemical) 
- 
The concentration is chosen based on a worse case basis. In the case of chemicals with 
legal limit, the maximum allowed concentration is chosen. In the case of no legal limit, 
the highest value of the recommended dosage range obtained in technical datasheet is 
set as the mean. 
- 
The statistical distribution is by default set to normal distribution. In the case of a legal 
limit, the mean is set as two standard deviation lower than the legal limit and three 
standard deviations away from the value zero. The mean and standard deviations are 
then derived from the system of two equation and two unknown displayed in equation 
15. This way, according to the proprieties of the normal distribution, 99.7% of the 
values will be positive and 95.4% of the values will be under the legal threshold 
(Pukelsheim, 1994). In the case of no legal limit the standard deviation is arbitrarily set 
as 10% of the mean.  
{
µ −3𝜎= 0          
       
µ + 2𝜎= 𝑙𝑖𝑚𝑖𝑡    
(𝐸𝑞 15) 
Where: 
 
µ is the mean in [gadditive/kgpolymer] 
 
𝜎 is the standard deviation in [gadditive/kgpolymer] 
 
𝑙𝑖𝑚𝑖𝑡  is the legal limit in [gadditive/kgpolymer] 
 
 
 
==PAGE:33==
 
 
34
One of the main goals of the algorithm developed in this project is to be as user-friendly as 
possible. This is done by making the interface simple using Microsoft Excel. The user can add new 
polymer types and chemical additives directly in the excel documents which will be read by the 
algorithm. The user can access the files containing the data and modify the different parameters. 
Furthermore, a dataset containing the plastic flows from Kawecki, Scheeder and Nowack (2018) is 
provided and read by the algorithm. 
The parameters can be accessed in different Excel files: 
• 
The main file contains the information filed by the user: the polymer and additive types and 
information regarding the plastic flow matrix. This way, the user can choose to either create a 
matrix or import data from another study and choose which polymer-additive couple he wishes 
to investigate. Links to the other parameter files as well as to the results file are also provided 
in the sheet to ease the navigation. 
It furthermore includes two sheets containing the data concerning the parameters for 
polymers and additives. In the case where the user wishes to add a polymer or additive type not 
included in these sheets, they can simply be edited and will be taken into account when running 
the algorithm. 
• 
A file containing the plastic flow matrices for several polymers, all taken from Kawecki, 
Scheeder and Nowac (2018). 
• 
A file containing the standard deviations corresponding to the normally distributed flows of the 
previously mentioned matrices. 
• 
A file containing the process’s parameters. For each process, the temperature, the time of stay, 
the dimensions of the material exiting the process and the model needed to calculate the 
migration are displayed. Alongside each parameter are displayed the probability density 
function to be used and the parameter defining the width of the distribution (e.g. standard 
deviation for mean distribution and shape for Weibull distribution). 
• 
A file containing the initial concentrations of various additives for all processes. it also displays 
the probability density function to be used and the parameter defining the width of the 
distribution. 
 
 
==PAGE:34==
 
 
35
3.3.2. 
Outputs 
The algorithm developed for this project calculates the flows of a specific additive embedded in 
a specific polymer in a system composed of several processes using migration models and several 
parameters. From this calculation, the algorithm can generate several outputs that allow the user to get 
detailed information on the situation. 
The algorithm provides a matrix showing the mass flows of the additive between each process. 
A new process is added corresponding to the total amount of additive lost by migration in each process. 
For all flows, the mean, median, fifth and ninety fifth quantiles are displayed in a table. Furthermore, 
for all processes, the proportion of migrated flow is displayed. It is calculated as the mass that has 
migrated from the product divided by the mass of additive present in the inflow and gives the share of 
additive that has migrated from the process. It allows to pinpoint the processes where notably high or 
low migration occurs. 
These results are displayed in an Excel file with several sheets for each polymer-additive couple 
that has been run by the algorithm. Each sheet contains the polymer flows, the substance flows with the 
mean, median, standard deviation, fifth and ninety fifth quantiles, the migrated share for each process 
with the standard deviations and the full matrix of substance flows. In the same file, an additional sheet 
allows to visualize the flows for a chosen polymer-additive couple. From this sheet, the flows can be 
displayed in a form that ease the export to a Sankey diagram software by showing all flows (i.e. even 
empty flows) next to the flow names. In this report, the diagrams are created using the software STAN 
(short for subSTance flow Analysis), developed by the Institute for Water Quality, Resource and Waste 
from the Vienna University of Technology in Austria (Cencic and Rechberger, 2008). In order to export 
the flows to the software, the flows must be named as “Fxxyy” for “From xx to yy”. The additional sheet 
in the result file allows to display the flows in such a form that can then be copy-pasted into STAN. 
3.4. 
Operations of the algorithm 
3.4.1. 
Preliminary steps 
The algorithm reads data on the form of a a matrix as shown in Table 6. In the case where the 
data is in a table form as in Table 5, a script is made to transform the data into a form usable by the 
algorithm.The script Make_Matrix.m reads an excel file containing data on plastic flows and transforms 
it in a matrix form. It does so by reading the process names in the file and attributing the flows to the 
right place in the plastic flow matrix. The script was built to read the dataset kindly provided by Delphine 
Kawecki in supplement of her article (Kawecki, Scheeder and Nowack, 2018). It could however be used 
for other datasets as well. It needs to be run prior to the main script in order to extract all the data in a 
specific file. 
==PAGE:35==
 
 
36
The data set needs to be of the form showed in Table 5 and will be transformed to a matrix as 
showed in Table 6. 
Table 5. Arrangement of the data set to be read by the Make_Matrix script 
From

Table 6. Arrangement of the matrix delivered by the Make_Matrix script

The algorithm calculating the additive flows is a Matlab script that can perform various 
operations as well as calling other scripts. The script is called Main.m and can be divided in several 
parts. 
The script Data_Import.m contains all the imports necessary for the main code to run. It reads 
from the excel files using the xlsread() function and assign the necessary parameters in vectors useable 
by the main script. 
An important step in the Main script is to modify the matrix by adding rows. Two lines are 
added to the plastic flow matrix. One line on the first row corresponding to the inflow of chemicals from 
outside of the system boundaries and one line on the last rows corresponding to the losses of chemicals 
from the diffusion phenomenon. As the flow matrix is square, adding a row requires adding a column. 
The two added rows are empty for the plastic flow layer but will contain the added and migrated 
chemicals in the chemical layer. 
 
 
==PAGE:36==
 
 
37
The next step is the initialization of the chemical flow matrix. The initial mass of chemical 
additive entering the system is needed to calculate all the other flows by propagation. The mass is based 
on the concentration of the additive in new products given in the parameters. The algorithm calculates 
the mass of additive required to satisfy that concentration. This step is realized for the manufacturing 
stage. For a process j manufacturing a plastic product, this translates to equation 16. 
𝐹𝑙𝑜𝑤𝑎𝑑𝑑𝑖𝑡𝑖𝑣𝑒 𝑡𝑜 𝑗 [𝑚𝑔] = 𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑐𝑒𝑛𝑡𝑟𝑎𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛𝑎𝑑𝑑𝑖𝑡𝑖𝑣𝑒[𝑚𝑔/𝑘𝑔] ∗∑𝐹𝑙𝑜𝑤𝑝𝑙𝑎𝑠𝑡𝑖𝑐,𝑓𝑟𝑜𝑚 𝑗[𝑘𝑔]
(𝑒𝑞 16) 
 
In the script, it is equivalent to the following line, with units’ adjustments from kT to mg 
Flow(1,j)= Processes(j-1,5)* sum(M_Plast(j,:)* 1e6; 
Where Flow (1, j) is the chemical flow to process j [mg] 
 
Processes(j-1,5) is the concentration of product manufactured in process j  
 
 
[mg additive/kg plastic] 
 
Sum (M_Plast (j,:)) is the total outflow of plastic from process j  [kg] 
3.4.2. 
Chemical flow calculation 
After calculating the initial chemical flows, the remaining flows can be calculated. 
To calculate the amount of chemical flowing in the system, the algorithm reads all the elements 
of the plastic flows matrix, written as M_Plast(i,j). In order to read through all the elements of the 
plastic flow matrix, the algorithm creates two loops in the following way. 
 
for j= (1: n-1) 
    for i= (1: n-1) 
        […] 
        […] 
    end 
end 
 
Each element of a column in the plastic matrix is an inflow of process j from process i. For each 
nonempty flow of a column, the algorithm calculates the amount of chemical that migrates from the 
plastic during the process due to the conditions within that process. This calculation is made by a 
migration function detailed in the above sections. As the migration functions calculate the migration for 
a specific object, the returned value needs to be multiplied by the amount of these objects making up the 
plastic flow. This number is defined with the mass of plastics in the flow, the dimensions of the product 
and the density of the polymer. It is calculated (equation 17) and written in Matlab as follows. 
==PAGE:37==
 
 
38
𝑛𝑏=
𝑚𝑝𝑙𝑎𝑠𝑡[𝑘𝑇] ∗1𝑒9
𝐴𝑟𝑒𝑎[𝑑𝑚2] ∗1𝑒4 ∗𝑑𝑒𝑝𝑡ℎ[𝑐𝑚] ∗𝑑𝑒𝑛𝑠𝑖𝑡𝑦[ 𝑚𝑔
𝑐𝑚3]
(𝑒𝑞 17) 
 
nb= 
M_Plast(i,j) 
1e9* 
/ 
((Processes(i,3)* 
Processes(i,4))* 1e4* Param_Polymer(1)); 
 
Where : 
M_Plast(i,j) is the mass flow from process I to process j [kT] 
Processes(i,3) and Processes(i,3) are the depth and Area of product coming from 
process in [cm] and [dm²] respectively. 
 
Param_Polymer(1) is the density of the polymer [mg/cm3] 
After all the elements in column j have been computed, the algorithm then sums the mass of 
chemical that stayed in the plastic for each inflow to calculate the total remaining chemical mass. This 
total remaining mass is then distributed throughout the outflows of the process proportionally to the 
amount of plastic in these outflows. This means that in all the outflows of a particular process the 
concentration in chemical is the same. Moreover, the algorithm will allocate to the Losses column all 
the lost chemical for all processes as illustrated in Figure 3 where for a process with one inflow, a share 
of the substance is released from the polymer and allocated to the Losses process. 
 
Figure 3. Process with one input flow and two output flows. The first one is a flow from Process 1 to another 
process in the system and the other one is the outflow resulting from the migration of the substance from the plastic 
products 
3.4.3. 
Assumptions 
There are several assumptions linked to this model. They do not concern neither the data nor the 
diffusion models but rather the way the flows are calculated and the technological level of the 
algorithms. The uncertainty brought by each of them will be evaluated in part 3.6 and discussed in part 
5.2.1. The assumptions are: 
==PAGE:38==
 
 
39
- 
To calculate the outflow of a process, each inflow has to be known. This translates into a 
horizontal calculation of flows and makes it impossible to add recycling loops or other feedback 
loops throughout the processes. The plastic layer taken from Kawecki Scheeder and Nowack 
(2018) is set in that manner. 
- 
Each process can have materials of different shapes and concentrations but will give as an 
outflow a single material with a defined shape and concentration. A shredder for instance can 
take in different plastic commodities such as plastic bottles, toys and boxes and will give as an 
outflow a uniform batch of shredded plastic containing a defined concentration of additive. 
- 
All the migrated additives from all the processes are placed in the same stock labelled Losses. 
It allows to quantify the total amount of migrated additive but does not discriminate between 
different areas or environments. This could be improved in further upgrades of the model. 
3.5. 
Data collection 
The aim of the data collection in this study was to find relevant information from which mean 
and standard deviation of parameters can be estimated and used to obtain relevant results as well as the 
resulting uncertainties. 
The first step was to identify the data. This is straightforward when they are clearly stated in the 
papers giving the formulae used in this project and do not change for different processes which is the 
case for physio-chemical properties of the polymer and the chemical. However, this is not the case for 
a number of other parameters. Some parameters can change from process to process as a function of 
variables such as temperature, time of stay and dimensions of the commodities. In this case, the goal is 
to assess what data can be relevant to describe each parameter. For instance, the temperature variable 
for plastic commodities used outdoors can be set as the average outdoor temperature and the temperature 
chosen for the manufacturing and recycling can be set as the melting temperature of the material. The 
choice of this data is detailed in Annex 
The second step is to find the data. Different approaches can be used. 
- 
For data concerning physicochemical properties of material, the researches where made within 
the scientific literature using search engines such as Google Scholar, ScienceDirect, PubChem 
and PubMed. When given as minimum and maximum values, the mean was used. 
- 
For data concerning products dimensions i.e. depth, width and length, online catalogues where 
used with the priority being given on catalogues containing multiple entries for each type of 
commodities. The underlying assumption concerning this research method is that more popular 
dimensions will be sold in more products while least popular dimensions will be found in a few 
products. This simulates the distribution of the dimensions and allows for a realistic estimation 
of the searched value. 
==PAGE:39==
 
 
40
- 
For temperature data, a data set containing per country temperatures was used. It encompasses 
all European countries for the year 2015 (Trading Economics, 2015). 
- 
For data specific to industrial processes, engineering articles or industrial guidelines where used. 
This concerns among others the temperatures of incineration process and landfills as well as the 
recommended thickness of plastic materials. 
- 
For some variables, no data could be found directly. Closely related data were assigned or in 
other cases, a combination of data was used. This is the case for instance of the other categories 
that were assigned the mean of the other commodities falling in the same category. 
The last step is to assign standard deviations and a fitting probability density function. As with 
the research of data, different approaches where used. 
- 
When available, the standard deviations where directly taken from the literature. This is the case 
for the plastic flow data that included uncertainties. However, the physiochemical properties of 
materials found in the literature rarely came with uncertainties which prevented the uncertainty 
analysis to apply to these parameters. 
- 
A significant amount of data was given with minimum and maximum values. In that case, the 
standard deviation was obtained by assuming a normal distribution and assuming that 95% of 
the values are encompassed within the max and the min values i.e. some extreme values can be 
located outside the min max values as the normal distribution allows an infinite range of values 
with a low probability. As the values less than two standard deviation away from the mean 
account for 95.45% of the set, the standard deviation can be approximated by the following 
formula (Pukelsheim, 1994) : 
𝜎= 𝑚𝑎𝑥−𝑚𝑖𝑛
4
(𝑒𝑞 18) 
Where:  
 
𝜎 is the standard deviation in the same unit as the mean 
max and min are the maximum and minimum values of the set, representing the 
boundaries at which 95% of the values are found. 
 
In the case of larger data sets of where a normal distribution could not be assumed, the 
distribution fitter tool in Matlab was used. It computes an array of data into a probability distribution of 
the user’s choice. For all the data sets, the Weibull distribution was used. The parameters given by the 
fitter tool are the scale parameter λ and the shape parameter k (Frechét, 1928) needed to describe the 
Weibull distribution. It is important to note that these values are not the same as the mean and the 
variance.  
==PAGE:40==
 
 
41
 
The high number of commodities as well as the large space boundary introduce high 
uncertainties. This is due to the fact that for a given category, the parameters need to be representative 
of a potentially large number of items included in that category, potentially differing in shapes and uses. 
Moreover, the conditions and uses can vary within the system definition, introducing more variations in 
the parameters. These uncertainties are materialized by a higher standard deviation giving a wider 
dispersion i.e. randomly generated numbers will have a higher probability of being distant from the 
mean value. 
3.6. 
Uncertainty analysis 
The measure of uncertainty was obtained using the Monte-Carlo method. It consists of running 
the algorithm multiple times using randomly generated parameters based on their statistical distribution. 
This allows to test a large number of possibility and returns for each flow an array of different values 
that can be analysed to study the uncertainty. For a specific parameter, the random values are generated 
following the statistical distribution, meaning that for a distribution with a low dispersion, the generated 
numbers will mostly gravitate around the mean value as shown in Figure 4 where values between 0 and 
1 give numbers gravitating around the mean value of the normal distribution.  
 
Figure 4. cumulative probability distribution of normal distribution with a mean of 10 and a standard 
deviation of 1. Lines correspond to values randomly generated between 0 and 1 giving values following the normal 
distribution. 
 
==PAGE:41==
 
 
42
 
In order to operate a Monte-Carlo simulation, a statistical distribution must be assigned to the 
parameters. Two statistical distribution were assigned in this study. 
• 
Normal distribution, defined by a mean µ and a standard deviation σ. By default, it is truncated 
to not get any negative values in parameters. 
• 
Weibull distribution, defined by a scale parameter λ and a shape parameter k (Frechét, 1928). 
In Matlab, the function makedist() is used to create a statistical distribution from these 
parameters and the function random() is used to create an array of random numbers following the chosen 
distribution. As shown in Figure 5, a greater precision is obtained by a larger amount of simulations 
meaning that the simulated parameters are better fitted to the parameter’s distribution. All the parameters 
are stored in a matrix with the number of columns being the number of parameters and the number of 
lines being the number of simulations selected.  
 
The algorithm calculating the flows of additive is then run multiple times, using each time the 
parameters from a different line of the matrix of randomly generated parameters. All the results are 
Figure 5. Approximation of a normal distribution of parameters mean=10 and standard 
deviation=1 with 10, 100 and 100000 simulations. 
==PAGE:42==
 
 
43
stored in a cell array containing the flow matrix of all the iterations. For a large amount of iteration, it is 
possible to describe the flows with a mean, a median and quantiles.  
4. Results 
The results for a polymer-additive couple are obtained by entering in the main Excel file the 
names of the polymer and additive and running the model on Matlab. The results are displayed in the 
results Excel sheet and can be showed as a Sankey diagram. The graphical results displayed in this 
section are made from the software STAN. 
From the model developed in this project, any pair of polymer and additive can be studied, 
provided that the data is sufficient. In this part, the results are displayed for Cadmium (Cd) within 
Polypropylene (PP) as an example with the remaining results in Appendix for Lead (Pb), diethylhexyl 
phthalate (DEHP), Bisphenol-A (BPA) and Irganox 1076. For all the mentioned additives, the initial 
concentration in the polymers were set as the maximum allowed concentration for application in 
polymers in Europe, shown in Table 7.However, as the legal values were not accessible for all additive, 
simplifications were made (see Table 7). All the values given in the result part for the additives are the 
median values of the simulated results and all the uncertainties are displayed as the standard deviations 
obtained from the Monte Carlo simulation. 
Table 7. Values used for the initial concentration of several substances. The values for mean and standard deviations 
were set so that the maximum allowed value would be two standard deviations away from the mean and that the value 
zero would be three standard deviations away from the mean.

4.1. 
Polymer flow 
Reviewing the polymer layer displayed in Figure 6 is essential in order to get a better 
understanding at the substance flow. Based on Kawecki, Scheeder and Nowack (2018), in 2014, the 
total import to the system of PP products or raw polymer amounted to 1,06E+04 ± 1,39E+03 kt/a while 
the total exports, in the form of product, raw polymer, waste or treated waste amounted to 6,52E+03 ± 
6,60E+02 kt/a. The mass of PP to landfill accounted for 4,03E+03 ± 5,59E+02 kt/a. The system proves 
to be balanced with the amount of inflow being equal to the sum of outflows and stocks. 
Looking at the overall system displayed in Figure 6, the import of primary matter for polymer 
production (9,32E+03 ± 1,38E+03 kt/a) and secondary material (7,62E+02 ± 1,49E+02) are transported 
to manufacturing (1,96E+03 ± 4,51E+02 kt/a for textile and 7,60E+03 ± 1,11E+03 kt/a for non-textile). 
The principal manufacturing processes are for packaging (3,22E+03 ± 5,97E+02 kt/a), technical textiles 
(1,68E+03 ± 3,76E+02 kt/a) and the other category (Principally directed toward household furniture and 
equipment, 2,02E+03 ± 4,00E+02 kt/a). On the waste management side, most of the product categories 
are collected in mixed wastes (6,22E+03 ± 5,73E+02 kt/a) to be sent to incineration (3,97E+03 ± 
5,38E+02 kt/a) and landfill (4,03E+03 ± 5,59E+02 kt/a). Alternatively, construction pipes, agricultural 
products, electrical equipment, automotive parts as well as part of packaging are largely collected in 
separated waste collection systems and used as secondary materials (1,22E+03 ± 1,65E+02 kt/a in total). 
==PAGE:44==
 
 
45
 
Figure 6. MFA system for Polypropylene in Europe in 2014. The values are displayed in kt/a and are taken 
from Kawecki, Scheeder and Nowack (2018) 
==PAGE:45==
 
 
46
Table 8  provides an overview of the largest flows of PP in the system. It shows a clear path of 
flow from the import of primary products to the production of polymer, the transport and the 
manufacturing of non-textile (Manuf in the table) and fibre production. On the waste management side, 
Table 8 shows that incineration is the most used way of disposing PP (3,97E+03 ± 6,17E+02 kt/a 
incinerated in total), and is primarily fuelled by mixed collection (ColMixed in the table, 3,05E+03 ± 
5,25E+02 kt/a). The total mass of additive entering the system is proportional to the initial concentration 
and the mass of polymer being manufactured.

4.2. 
Additives 
Cadmium is used in plastic manufacturing usually as a pigment added in the form of a powder. 
In Europe, its use is restricted to concentrations lower than 100µg/g for both packaging and non-
packaging products (European Commission, 2011). The MFA diagram displayed in Figure 7 shows the 
route of cadmium in the system. The total mass of additive entering the system amounted to 6,00E+11 
± 1E+11 mg/a for Cd, and the biggest flows are owed to the initial inflow of additive to be mixed to the 
polymer in the manufacturing processes. From these flows, the highest ones are the inflow to packaging 
production (2,06E+11 ± 7,36E+10 mg/a), to the production of the category other plastic products 
(1,33E+11 ± 4,97E+10 mg/a), to the technical textiles production (1,11E+11 ± 4,10E+10 mg/a) and the 
automotive parts production (6,67E+10 ± 2,53E+10 mg/a). From the production to the use phase, the 
largest flows were from the other plastic products category to household plastics (7,77E+10 ± 3,37E+10 
mg/a), from packaging manufacturing to the consumer other category (7,07E+10 ± 2,94E+10 mg/a) and 
==PAGE:46==
 
 
47
from the automotive parts manufacturing to automotive parts (4,93E+10 ± 2,14E+10 mg/a). Finally, in 
some cases, the release of Cd in the environment accounted for some of the largest flows. In particular 
cadmium migrated from the use phase of household plastics (7,12E+10 ± 3,32E+10 mg/a), automotive 
parts (4,93E+10 ± 2,14E+10 mg/a) and consumer other (4,52E+10 ± 2,40E+10 mg/a). 
==PAGE:47==
 
 
48
 
Figure 7. MFA system for Cadmium in Europe in 2014. The flows are displayed in mg/a and are calculated 
using the algorithm developed in this project. 
 
==PAGE:48==
 
 
49
 
While the substance flows give information on the total flows of cadmium in the value chain, 
they need to be put into perspective as it is easy to observe that the largest flows of cadmium are often 
the same as the largest PP flows as the mass flows of additives are proportional to the mass flow of 
polymer. To get a clearer view, one can calculate content of additive – i.e. the mass of additive per mass 
of polymer - for all flows in the system.  
 
 
==PAGE:49==
 
 
50
Table 10 shows the highest mass fractions in mg of cadmium per kg of PP. The highest values 
are the ones from the import of materials to the manufacturing processes but are not taken into account 
at this stage as the polymer and additive are not mixed together and these amounts are therefore not 
mass fractions. As shown in  
 
 
==PAGE:50==
 
 
51
Table 10, all mass fractions remain below 60 mg/kg (i.e. the initial concentration of additive), 
which means that there is no accumulation of cadmium in the value chain. Furthermore, the mass 
fraction seems to decrease progressively in the value chain due to cadmium being released. The mass 
fraction is dependent on the migration taking place previously in the value chain, as a high migration in 
a previous process lowers the amount of additive, but leaves the mass of polymer unchanged, thus 
lowering the mass fraction.

To get a better understanding of the migration mechanism, the ratio of release is calculated. 
These results communicate on the ability of the additive to be released from the polymer in each process. 
Table 12 shows the highest migration shares for all the additives calculated. In the case of Cd, 
it appears that all the ten largest ratios show 100% of the cadmium migrated from the product meaning 
that all cadmium present in the materials have been released. These processes include for the 
manufacturing phase: processes pipes and ducts, clothing, automotive and household textile, for the use 
phase processes: other plastic products, agricultural other, building textiles, furniture, household 
plastics, consumer films, consumer bags and cosmetics and landfill. For some processes with migration 
levels above 90%, the uncertainty is low as almost all the simulations on the Monte Carlo method will 
result in high migration. This is due to the fact that for these processes, the parameters are extreme in 
regard to the migration model. 
Alternatively,  
 
==PAGE:52==
 
 
53
Table 11 shows the lowest migrating shares, i.e. the processes in which little migration occurs. 
Most of these processes are part of the waste management systems, with all recycling processes showing 
a less than 5% migration apart from packaging recycling processes that shows 18% migration. These 
results will be further examined in the discussion section. 
 
 
==PAGE:53==
 
 
54
Figure 8 to Figure 13 show the migration rates of the additives calculated for several flows 
represented using box plots. The box plots are drawn using all values generated using the MonteCarlo 
method with 10 000 simulations. On each box, the median is shown by the central mark and the 25th 
and 75th percentiles are indicated by the bottom and top edge respectively. This way, the box covers 
50% of the values. The whiskers fences are calculated as the interquartile range multiplied by 1.5 (See 
Eq 20 and Eq 21) (Råde and Westergren, 1990). The outliers are the values outside the whiskers and are 
plotted individually by a ‘+’ symbol. The flows to be plotted were chosen as the highest ratios for Cd in 
PP that had a median value inferior to 90%. As such, the goal is not to show the highest ratios in a ranked 
form (this information is displayed in Table 12) but rather to show the differences brought by the type 
of additive.

The figures show that the MonteCarlo method produces a lot of outliers, with all displayed ratios 
containing outliers both above and under the median. Moreover, the type of additive plays a role in the 
magnitude of the ratio, with the median clearly different for all additive types. For all ratios, the values 
can be ranked as Cd>Pb>BPA>DEHP>Oct with an exception for Pb and BPA that show similar medians 
for all processes.
