==PAGE:60==
 
 
61
5. Discussion 
5.1. 
Discussion on the results 
5.1.1. 
Substance flow 
The substance flow of cadmium embedded in polypropylene in Europe starts with the addition 
of cadmium as a raw material in the manufacturing stage. At this point, all categories of products are 
mixed with the additive in the same proportions i.e. 60mg/kg in the case of Cd. From this point, the 
additive follows the same path as the polymer layer. Due to the initial concentration being equal for all 
product categories, large polymer flows result in large additive flows. The only distinction with the 
polymer layer is the fact that in each process in the value chain, a fraction of additive can be released 
from the polymer matrix due to the phenomenon of migration. The magnitude of the release depends on 
the polymer and additive properties but also on the dimensions of the plastics and on the conditions 
within the process. This way, two different products going to the same process will show different levels 
of migration and two flows from the same process going to two different processes will show different 
levels of migration as well.  
The largest release ratios are observed in the use phase, with up to 100% migrated in the case 
of cadmium. This high level of migration is due in part to the limitation of the migration functions in 
the case of extreme values of the parameters. This is in particular the case for the OECD function that 
tends to result in high levels of release in the case of thickness lower than the millimetre. 
As observed in the result section, the processes in the waste management phase show for the 
most part low shares of migration, meaning that the products in these processes do not release much 
substance compared to how much they contain. This can be due to the fact that the OECD function used 
to model the migration in air contact does not take temperature into account when calculating the mass 
of substance released. The temperature parameter is embedded in the diffusion coefficient which, in the 
case of the OECD model only takes into account the molar mass of the migrant. In the case of waste 
management processes, which include recycling processes, the high temperatures would be expected to 
cause higher levels of substance release. 
Concerning the end of life processes, the major substance flows are the one going from mixed 
waste collection to incineration and landfill. The mass of cadmium present in the incinerated plastic 
shows very little migration while in the case of landfilled plastic waste, all additive is released. The 
former can be explained the same way as for the waste management processes, with temperature not 
affecting migration while the later can be explained by the 5000 ± 2500 years timescale associated with 
the landfill process. 
==PAGE:61==
 
 
62
The comparison of release ratios between five additives shows the large differences in the 
release for different substances. The main reason for this difference is the difference of molar mass 
across the substances. A low molar mass means that the compound will easily travel through the polymer 
matrix and thus migrate faster. 
The implications for human health involve products in contact with food, drinks or the human 
skin as well as contamination from breathing. The large majority of studies concern migration to food 
and skin contact. In case of contact or ingestion, the consequences for human health depend on the 
additive. In the case of cadmium, it is classified as a human carcinogen and shows chronic effects to 
lungs and kidneys of the populations affected (WHO Task Group on Environmental Health Criteria for 
Cadmium. et al., 1992). This project focuses on the flows of additives on a large scale and it is therefore 
not its aim to assess the effects of the selected additives on a local scale. However, it would be possible 
to use the model developed in this project to assess the exposure to certain additives on a large scale. 
The implications concerning the environment are the accumulation in nature of hazardous 
chemicals in concentrations higher than natural ones in the atmosphere, soil or water (Harrison, 1999). 
The consequences for the ecosystems might vary depending on the location and type of additive. 
Moreover, other processes not studied in this project might increase the environmental exposure to 
hazardous substances such as the degradation of plastic products into microplastics via littering or wear 
off. The model developed in this project could be used to study the effects on the environment by 
coupling it with models for ecotoxicology. 
In order to decrease the exposure to potentially hazardous substances, several options can be 
looked at. Strengthening the legal limitation of concentration in the case of hazardous substances is very 
effective to lower the total mass of substance released from plastics and thus to lower the public and the 
environment exposure to these substances. This option is especially relevant for substances with lower 
molar mass as they have shown a higher potential of release. Another option can be to lower the lifetime 
of plastic products. This option is only useful in the case of new legislations as it allows to discard 
products containing high concentrations of hazardous substances and replace them with products 
containing safer levels of substances. 
 
 
==PAGE:62==
 
 
63
5.1.2. 
Comparison with litterature 
Several studies exist covering the issue of additive migration from plastic materials. However, 
these studies focus on specific materials and substances and thus hardly cover all relevant polymers and 
substances. For instance, there are no studies focusing on the migration of cadmium from polypropylene. 
Furthermore, these studies cover only a few situations. While most studies focused on food contact some 
studies considered the use phase, for toys for example (Turner, 2018). 
Concerning the comparison between experimental data available in the literature and the results 
obtained from the model developed for this project, the principal barrier is the variety of settings used 
in laboratory experiments. As the model encompasses all products of a polymer type in Europe in 2014, 
this variety is lost and only global numbers obtained by average parameters are obtained. Beldì et al. 
(2012), in a study on the migration of the antioxidant Irganox 1076 from polyethylene, showed that the 
level of migration of the additive is linearly correlated to the fat content of the food in contact with the 
product. In the present model, this factor is not taken into account and thus the comparison cannot be 
fully pertinent.  
It is possible to assess the results of this study not by comparing the global results with 
laboratory experiments but by testing the individual migration models to laboratory experiments. These 
comparisons were already made in the development of the models. In the case of the model taken from 
Simoneau (2010), several validation experiments were conducted. Figure 14 shows a comparison in the 
case of diffusion coefficient, calculated as the square root of the model estimation to experiential data 
for 415 experiments. The square root is used because “the migration rate is directly proportional to the 
square root (SQR) of Dp” (Piringer and Hinrichs, 2004). This figure shows that the model gave values 
1.4 to 25 time higher than the experimental value in approximately ¾ of the experiments. It also shows 
that a small proportion of the experiments showed results inferior to the modelled values which 
highlights the overestimating character of the model. Figure 14Figure 15 shows the comparison of 
modelled and experimental data for the additive PM/REF 61600 in various PP samples in contact with 
olive oil and for two different situations. The same conclusion can be made as for Figure 14 as the model 
shows to be overestimating the experimental results in all but two comparisons, while giving an accurate 
result with less than 300% overestimation. 
==PAGE:63==
 
 
64
 
Figure 14. Histogram of the Square Root of the Ratios between Diffusion Coefficients estimated with the 
"Refined Equation from 1999" and 415 experimental Diffusion Coefficients in LLDPE and LPDE (Taken from 
Piringer and Hinrichs (2004) 
 
Figure 15. Comparison of modelled and experimental data for the migration of additive PM/REF 61600 into 
olive oil (Taken from O’Brien and Cooper (2001). Each polymer tested is a different sample used for migration tests. 
 
 
==PAGE:64==
 
 
65
As mentioned above, comparing the modelled results to observed or measured values brings a 
problem of scale. A way to perform this comparison in the case of release of additive to the environment 
could be to measure the release of the chosen additive to the environment for a specific additive, several 
materials and several conditions. These experimental values could then be upscaled to the continental 
scale. However, this method would be limited by the large amount of experiments required to estimate 
the release of additive in a large variety of products and waste management systems. From this 
observation, it seems crucial to understand the migration models individually to assess their 
representativeness. 
5.2. 
Reflexions on the model 
5.2.1. 
Parameters 
While several parameters are taken into account in the migration models used in the algorithm, 
these models cannot fully depict all situations. Both models use a coefficient of diffusion to estimate the 
rate at which diffusion occur within the polymer. In the case of the Simoneau model, polymer specific 
parameters, the molecular mass of the migrant, the density of the migrant and the polymer and the 
temperature are taken into account. In the case of the OECD model, the authors recommend an 
approximation from Reynier, Dole and Feigenbaum (2002), taking only the molecular weight of the 
additive into account. 
Other parameters influence the coefficient of diffusion such as pressure, the porosity of the 
polymer structure or the viscosity of the fluid in contact with the plastic product (Cussler, 1997). 
Reynier, Dole and Feigenbaum (2002) discuss the role of the molecular structure of the migrant. For 
two molecules of the same molecular weight, an elongated structure would allow one to migrate more 
easily from the polymer matrix than a molecule with a spherical shape. From this observation, they 
defend that other parameters could be taken into account such as the minimum cross section, the shape, 
the interactions with the polymeric matrix or the flexibility of the molecule. 
Furthermore, in both models used in the algorithm, the parameters are assumed constant during 
each process. However, in particular in situations of long timescale, the parameters are expected to 
fluctuate. In the case of temperature, the fluctuations are both spatial - Europe includes different climates 
- and temporal - with temperature varying between seasons. This leads to the use of large standard 
deviation to simulate the fluctuating conditions. As underlined in Hahladakis et al. (2018) and Turner 
(2019), plastic products can also be subject to change in their structure. This can be done by scraping, 
breaking or wear and tear.  
==PAGE:65==
 
 
66
5.2.2. 
Release of additive 
In this project, all the migrated substance is assigned to a single category labelled Losses. This 
aggregation of released chemical to a single category of ecosystem does not allow for an in-depth 
analysis of the effects of each chemical type to the environment. In the case of cadmium, Williams and 
Harrison (1984) showed that the incineration of waste is one of the main source of airborne Cd, primarily 
from plastic additives. In the case of human injection, although migration to food and drink is considered 
in this study and investigated in numerous articles (Cai, Nthoiwa and Ge, 2014; Poovarodom et al., 
2014; Whitt et al., 2016), ingestion from other products such as toys from infant’s mouthing is an 
important case that could be investigated with the model presented in this project by adding new 
categories such as the use phase of toys, a mouthing or eating process.  
5.2.3. 
Use of static MFA 
This study is a static MFA as it only assesses the release of additive from a polymer within a 
year. However, it accounts for the lifetime in the products considered which involves an important 
assumption. In order to simulate the lifetime of the products, the model assumes that the mass flows of 
plastic are constant over the full lifetime of each product. This is done primarily because the MFA model 
on polymer products used as the polymer layer (Kawecki, Scheeder and Nowack, 2018) did not account 
for the accumulation or depletion of stocks but rather considered a null variation of stocks. This means 
that their assumption is that the plastic products newly manufactured replace the discarded plastic 
products. And in other words that the production of plastic products does not increase each year. The 
calculation of the release of additive is based on the solution of the second Fick’s law and thus on the 
integration of the concentration over time. This means that for a given process, a mathematically correct 
way to calculate the release of additive would be to calculate for each cohort of product their associated 
solution of the integral. For instance, the release from newly produced products would be calculated by 
solving the integral from the year zero to year one, the products created the past year from year one to 
two etc. This method would give more importance to the new products as the production of plastic tends 
to increase in Europe. Along with the change in polymer production, the additive concentrations in 
newly manufactured plastic products is not taken into account in this static MFA approach. In this study, 
the same initial concentration is allocated for all cohorts meaning that it is assumed that the initial 
concentration of a specific additive to a specific product is the same in 2014 and 2004. In reality, this 
value is suspected to change in particular due to legal regulations and these regulations tend to lower the 
allowed additive concentration. 
 
==PAGE:66==
 
 
67
5.2.4. 
Limitations of the migration models 
The two migration functions used in this project are the main components of the model, making 
it important to analyse their performance in assessing the release of additives from individual products. 
As displayed in the results section, the ratio of additive released to additive present in a product can 
reach high values, including 100% for some processes. This observation calls for a thorough review of 
the two models. 
The model based on the work of Simoneau (2010) is explicitly designed for food contact 
conditions. A sensitivity analysis allows to investigate the effects of the most relevant parameters on the 
migration of cadmium from a single piece of polypropylene. Unless stated otherwise, the sensitivity 
analysis is set on a single piece of Polypropylene of 1 m² area of contact and 1 cm thickness in conditions 
of 300 K and timescale of 5 years with an initial concentration of 1000 mg/kg.  
Figure 1 shows the ratio of released cadmium to cadmium present in the product with the 
temperature varying from 0 to 500K. The release is stagnant from 0 to 200K and then reaches its 
maximum value before 300K. This illustrates the short temperature window, the model being dedicated 
to analysis under normal indoor temperatures. Figure 2 shows the ratio of the mass of cadmium released 
from a product compared to the mass of cadmium present in the product with the timescale varying from 
0 to 10 years. As expected, the release decreases with time, reaching 50% of its maximum value in 0.1 
year and 90% in two years. Figure 3 and Figure 4 show the ratio of the mass of cadmium released from 
a product compared to the mass of cadmium present in the product with the timescale varying from 0 to 
10 years for a temperature of 100 and 500K respectively. This indicates that the timescale does not affect 
the migration for a temperature outside the range of 200 to 300, further illustrating the short amplitude 
of the parameters’ relevance.  
Figure 5 shows shows the ratio of the mass of cadmium released from a product compared to 
the mass of cadmium present in the product with the thickness of contact varying from 0 to 100 cm. It 
shows that the ratio of cadmium migrated from the product is increasing exponentially until reaching 
100% when the thickness decreases. 
==PAGE:67==
 
 
68
 
Figure 16. Effect of temperature in Kelvin on the ratio of chemical migrated calculated with the Simoneau 
model. 
 
Figure 17. Effect of timescale on the ratio of chemical migrated calculated with the Simoneau model. The X 
axis is in a logarithmic scale

Figure 19. Effect of timescale on the ratio of chemical migrated calculated with the Simoneau model. The 
temperature is set at 500K 
==PAGE:69==
 
 
70
 
 
Figure 20. Effect of thickness on the ratio of chemical migrated calculated with the Simoneau model. The X 
axis is in a logarithmic scale 
The OECD migration model, based on the emission scenario document on plastic additives 
(Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development, 2014) is a generic model assessing the 
release of additive to air. The sensitivity analysis performed here investigates the effects of the most 
relevant parameters on the migration of cadmium from a single piece of polypropylene. Unless stated 
otherwise, the sensitivity analysis is set on a single piece of Polypropylene of 1m² area of contact and 1 
cm thickness in conditions of 300 K and timescale of 5 years with an initial concentration of 1000 mg/kg.  
Figure 5 shows the ratio of the mass of cadmium released to the mass of cadmium present in the 
product with the thickness of contact varying from 0 to 10 cm. It shows that the ratio of cadmium 
migrated from the product is increasing exponentially until climbing to values larger than 100% of the 
total amount of cadmium present in the product. Figure 6 shows the ratio of released cadmium to 
cadmium present in the product with the timescale varying from 0 to 100 years. It shows that the ratio 
reaches 100% after 20 years and is increasing indefinitely. These observations likely lead to an 
overestimation of migration. This is especially the case for thickness as numerous plastic products have 
a low thickness. Plastic films for instance are less than a millimetre thick. 
 
==PAGE:70==
 
 
71
 
The thickness and timescale parameters show the limitations of the equation used in the OECD 
model. The simple equation governing the released mass brings strong limitation in the magnitude of 
the parameters and omit important parameters such as temperature. The report from the Organisation 
for Economic Co-operation and Development (2014) mentions a limitation on the validity of the model 
at 20% of the additive migrated but due to the lack of other more representative models, the lack of 
justification in the OECD report for the 20% value and the simulation under worse case conditions, a 
limit at 100% of the migrated additive was put in place. 
 
Figure 21. Effect of thickness on the ratio of chemical migrated calculated with the OECD model. The X axis 
is in a logarithmic scale 
==PAGE:71==
 
 
72
 
Figure 22. Effect of timescale on the ratio of chemical migrated calculated with the OECD model. 
 
 
==PAGE:72==
 
 
73
 
5.2.5. 
Modular aspect of the model 
The algorithm built in this project uses migration model to calculate the release of additive for 
each process in a system. While the theory of diffusion based on the Fick’s laws is relatively well 
understood, the solution to these equations are more difficult to derive and migration models are still 
scarce. In this project, due to limited resources, only the two migration models described in the report 
were considered. The algorithm built in this project was developed to be modular up to a certain extent. 
As such, the data can be accessed and changed via Microsoft Excel and new migration models can easily 
be added by creating a function in Matlab taking as input the data and giving as an output the mass 
migrated from a product.  
Several aspects should be taken into consideration before inserting a new migration model in 
the algorithm. First, the parameters need to be the same as the one provided in the excel sheet. New 
parameters could potentially be added but adding columns to the Excel sheet could lead to bugs in the 
execution of the algorithm. Second, the model to be used for each parameter is indicated by a number 
in the processes parameters sheet. A new number would need to be filled for the according processes 
and this number should be stated in the algorithm as follows:  
 
“if number==x 
 
Mass_migrated=new_model (Parameters) 
end 
In the future, new models could arise with different possible applications. This way, the 
limitations outlined in part 5.2.4 can be surpassed in the case where new additive release model are 
developed. 
 
 
==PAGE:73==
 
 
74
5.3. 
Recommendations for future work 
The observations throughout the discussion part raise several tracks to improve the model 
developed in this project. First and foremost, an improvement in the migration model would allow to 
obtain more precise result and improve the strength of the results and conclusions drawn by this model. 
Using this model with multiple migration models would also allow to improve the specificity of the 
migration. For example, different specific models for outdoor commodities, underground products, 
landfilling or recycling processes would greatly lower the uncertainty for the processes corresponding 
to these situations. Furthermore, developing a method allowing to allocate several migration models to 
each process would allow to consider more complex situation. This would be interesting in the case of 
products being in contact with different surroundings such as agricultural pipes that are in contact with 
soil, air and water. 
An interesting next step to this project could be to study the possible routes to different 
ecosystems. This could allow to study the amounts being absorbed through skin contact and ingestion 
or to soil and oceans via leaching for instance. In relation with the wear and tear of plastic product, it 
would allow to get a better understanding of the effects of smaller plastic pieces and microplastics in the 
environment. Although it would give greater information, it is expected to make this model much more 
complex as it would require using several models for each process as well as calculations of transfer 
coefficient for several ecosystems considered. 
Last, a dynamic approach would be an interesting next step to this project. Dynamic material 
flow analysis studies the flows of material across several time frames. It allows to reconstitute the change 
in behaviour of material through time, the effect of varying inflows of material on the overall flows, the 
possible feedback loops of material from year to year and the possible accumulations of stock in 
processes. A dynamic MFA would require an extensive research for historical data on plastic production 
and additive concentrations in various products. It would also require a different modelling approach 
that would combine the one conceived for this project along with a consideration on stock and parameter 
change. 
 
 
==PAGE:74==
 
 
75
6. Conclusion 
 
The model built in this project uses the material flow analysis methodology coupled with migration 
modelling to calculate the flow of any additive embedded in any polymer. A review on the diffusion 
phenomenon as well as on the mathematical models calculating migration in polymers was performed 
to develop a model that can calculate the release of substance for several processes within a system. The 
results were obtained for five additives embedded in polypropylene and were detailed for cadmium 
embedded in polypropylene. 
The results for cadmium in propylene with an initial concentration of 60 mgCd/kgPP for all polypropylene 
products showed to involve an overall input of to 6,00E+11 ± 1E+11 mg/a, and an equal output. In this 
output, 9.39E+09 ± 4.47E+09 mg/a is from trade of plastic materials, 8.73E+08 ± 6.90E+08 mg/a is 
from the outflow of plastic waste for secondary use and 4.86E+11 ± 6.30E+10 mg/a is from cadmium 
released from plastic products. The ratios of cadmium released from plastic products showed to range 
from 0% in the case of Y to 100% in the case of several processes, including use phase of different 
commodities and landfill. The low shares of migration are due to conditions not favourable to the release 
of substance such as a low timescale or large thickness as well as migration models not taking into 
account parameters that are suspected to increase the release such as temperature in the case of the 
function modelling the release with air contact. These results show that the model developed in for this 
project is successful at calculating the behaviour of plastic additive in plastic products. However, the 
results need to be taken carefully as the model contains several limitations that lower the level of 
confidence. 
The release of substance from plastic products is allocated to a single process labelled Loses. This 
doesn’t allow for a differentiation of the destination of the substance or it’s route after being released 
from the polymer. However, the high ratios of release in several processes suggest a strong potential of 
increased exposure to chemical additives. From this observation, the main approach to decrease the 
exposure to potentially hazardous substances is to strengthen the limitations and use alternative 
substances. This is particularly relevant for substances with low molar mass that can be released more 
easily from plastic products. 
The aim of this project was to develop a tool that has the potential to be handled by any user. As such, 
all the data sets are available and easy to modify and a tool to create system from plastic flows data is 
also provided. A tool cannot be developed to a professional standard in the conditions of a master’s 
thesis and the emphasis was put on the choice of the mathematical models used, their limitations and 
the limitations of the model in general. Moreover, the model was built so that the migration functions, 
==PAGE:75==
 
 
76
calculating the release of additive from plastic products, could easily be modified or replaced and that 
new ones could be added in the event of new migration models being developed.

Appendix A : The positive roots of the trigonometric identity tan 𝑞𝑛= −𝛼∗𝑞𝑛. 
It is worth to compare the analytical results from the eaquations given by Piringer and Baner 
(2000) as they can hold uncertainties. Table 13 show the values given in Piringer and Baner (2000) and 
Table 14 Shows the values obtained with the formulas given in the same paper. Table 15 gives the 
percentage difference between the two. It shows a high difference, up to 16% for α=1,00 and n=1. 
Overall, it seems that the high differences affect all the values for α except small and high ones and are 
mostly observed on small values of n.

==PAGE:92==
 
 
93
Appendix C: Raw data for the flows of Cd, Pb, BPA, DEHP and Irganox 1076 embedded 
in PP and LDPE 
 
In this part, all the flows for Cd, Pb, BPA, DEHP and Irganox 1076 embedded in PP and LDPE 
are displayed. For each additive, a short description taken from is provided. 
• 
Cadmium (Cd):  
o Heavy metal usually used as pigment. Used as heat and UV stabilizer in PVC (Hansen et 
al., 2013). 
o Can pose severe risks to human health and to biodiversity. Cadmium intoxication leads to 
kidney, bone, and pulmonary damages (Godt et al., 2006). 
 
• 
Lead (Pb): 
o Heavy metal usually used as pigment. Used as heat and UV stabilizer in PVC (Hansen et 
al., 2013). 
o Can pose severe risks to human health and to biodiversity. Almost no body function that is 
not affected by lead toxicity (Wani, Ara and Usmani, 2015) 
 
• 
Bisphenol A (BPA) 
o Monomer used to manufacture several polymers (polycarbonate, epoxy resin, unsaturated 
polyester resin). Antioxidant. Used as PVC additive 
o BPA is theoretically not present in PP and LDPE. Therefore the results are given solely as 
indicative of their behaviour in the case of usage with the concentration cited in the results 
part(Hansen et al., 2013) 
o “BPA exhibits toxic, endocrine, mutagenic and carcinogenic effect in living organisms and 
is supposed to elevate risk of obesity, diabetes and heart disease in humans.”(Michałowicz, 
2014) 
 
• 
Bis(2-ethylhexyl) phthalate (DEHP) 
o Pasticiser mainly used in PVC. DEHP is used to manufacture flexible parts in plastic 
objects. And is typically used in concentrations about 30% (w/w) 
o DEHP is theoretically not present in PP and LDPE. Therefore the results are given solely 
as indicative of their behaviour in the case of usage with the concentration cited in the results 
part (Hansen et al., 2013) 
o DEHP “exhibits low toxicity from acute (short-term) and chronic (long-term) exposures. 
Acute exposure to large oral doses of DEHP can cause gastrointestinal distress in humains.” 
(EPA, 2000) 
 
• 
Irganox® 1076  
o Antioxidant with long term stabilisation, protecting the polymer from heat and oxidation 
degradations (Alin and Hakkarainen, 2011). (SpecialChem and BASF, 2019) 
o It is not listed as a toxic or carcinogenic compound (Pesticide Action Network North 
America, 2019)
